## Reply 36 — participant_029 · 2019-11-06 · post-25986644

## Reply 37 — participant_011 · 2019-11-06 · post-25986646

<!-- artifact:quote:start -->
> Asiak said: Please don't make smaller de-jure kingdoms like CK2 started to have like Trebizond and Epirius. And please can we re-evaluate the entire need for de-jure empires, really there should only be a few like Rome, Persia, Tibet maybe one in India. I just never saw "players wanted more empires to create" as a valid reason for having such a-historical empires as the Wendish Empire. Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
I think de-jure empires should only be limited to those that exist or have existed within a milennia of the game's start date. So things like Abbasids, Umayyad, ERE (Empire of the Romans/Byzantium), WRE (should be its own empire you can reform imho) do get a pass, but ahistorical stuff or things like Babylon don't. Also, if you hold the titles of ERE and WRE both, you should be able to take a decision to combine them into a unified de-jure Roman Empire. As for newly-emergent empires, imho when a country grows large enough, it should unlock a decision for its ruler to crown him/herself as emperor/empress and in doing so form a titular empire, and that empire could then become de-jure through kingdom drift over time, which depending on the game rules would likely happen over 50-150 years of its continued, stable existence. The decision could require, say: - Holds at least 1 Kingdom-tier title - Realm size is at least x many counties - Dynasty is at least <this> prestigous - OR = { { has_religious_head_approval } {prestige >= 5000} } Empire-tier titles without any de-jure titles underneath would be destroyed if not held by anyone for over 50 years.

## Reply 46 — participant_033 · 2019-11-06 · post-25986669

<!-- artifact:quote:start -->
> Servancour said: One of the most notable changes is how we handle Baronies. In CK2, Counties were the smallest entity we had on the map, a province if you will, with several Baronies represented through the interface of the County view. In CK3, we took the next logical step and made Baronies into their own provinces. We have been able to create a map with much more granularity and better accuracy. Most Counties will normally consist of two to five Baronies, with some exceptions. The amount of provinces will be noticeable when waging war, as it offers a larger degree of movement for you armies (more on that in the future). Before you all go nuts about playable baronies: No. You cannot play as a Baron. The lowest playable rank will still be that of a Count. The emphasis will therefore be on the Counties rather than the individual Baronies. As such, Baronies exist with a few things in mind. For example, they can never leave a county. This means Counties stay the same over time, avoiding weird splits where a single barony goes independent or to another realm (reducing that hideous border-gore ever-so-slightly). Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
More provinces! Great! The second part however needs a little bit of clarification: "Baronies can never leave a county" - I get this part, it essentially means "de jure" counties always stay the same. "Avoiding weird splits where a single barony goes independent or to another realm" - I suppose this means there's now way to inherit a barony that is in a county that's not part of your own realm? I get that this was a pain in CK2 because not all subholdings were visible on the map. But in CK3 this (1) would actually be playable since it would be clearly visible on the map and (2) it would be more historical (yes, bordergore was extremely prevalent in medieval times. Apologies to all bordergore haters). I, for one, would like to be able to inherit single baronies outside my realm, but that might just be the historian in me talking. It would also provide a great Casus Belli. Sidenote: Speaking of Casus Belli, I hope it will be possible to conquer more than what you declare for, not like currently in CK2. Maybe depending on your warscore you will be able to take more, but the more you take that was not part of the wargoal the more it costs, scaling up. For example if you take the wargoal, then the second thing you take costs twice as much as normal, the third 3 times as much, etc. This way you can take more than you initially wanted, but it's not possible to completely annex the enemy's realm.

## Reply 55 — participant_038 · 2019-11-06 · post-25986705

<!-- artifact:quote:start -->
> Servancour said: The former. You cannot transfer barony vassals out of a given county, and the county holder will always be that baron's liege. Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
How will we be able to simulate things like Imperial Free Cities, Imperial Immediacy, and other parts of history where cities became direct vassals of the crown in order to not have to pay taxes to both the crown and their liege? It'd be weird to have to make a city a county's primary holding/barony (I assume CK3 will still use this system) just to be able to have it as a direct vassal, as that would also turn the other baronies in the county into that city's vassals, which Free Cities (and similar cities outside of the HRE) did not have.

## Reply 56 — participant_039 · 2019-11-06 · post-25986708

<!-- artifact:quote:start -->
> Servancour said: Speaking of terrain, we have several different terrain types spread out across the map. Instead of having a single terrain spread out across large areas of the map, we differentiate between similar terrain types by separating them, such as Forest and Taiga, or Plains and Drylands. Not only does it make the map look and feel distinct in different parts of the world, they also have a different impact on gameplay. Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
Having varied terrain is a good first step. Now please introduce a system of economic goods (for example, Imperator) so that provinces don't all feel identical in terms of economic output and there is some meaningful interaction in terms of trade and diplomacy. A count who controls wine-making counties in Burgundy should be living in very different circumstances from an earl managing pastures in York. People who shear sheep in England should have a vested interest in maintaining good relations with the cloth-making industry in Flanders.

## Reply 76 — participant_016 · 2019-11-06 · post-25986795

<!-- artifact:quote:start -->
> fr-rein said: But we can still inherit baronies from other counties and detach them as our direct vassals, right? Like it may happen in CK2 atm. Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
<!-- artifact:quote:start -->
> glennandre22 said: May I also ask if the Baron's themselves are characters like how they worked in CK2, or do they work as a community or "building" - in an effort to reduce the overpopulation chances? Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
No. You can still inherit baronies from your own counties, but not from a county belonging to someone else. If such a situation would happen, it will be inherited by the county holder instead. They are still very much held by actual characters. We just put less emphasize on them.

## Reply 138 — participant_079 · 2019-11-06 · post-25987118

I've got to say, while I'm liking the overall look of it, I do have one major gripe: Baronies inseparable from counties (presumably hardcoded). This creates so many issues while solving ... exactly one (bordergore) that I really cannot see the benefit of locking baronies to being always subject to the same county. Not only is this ahistorical (the HRE has been mentioned), but it also creates issues with Holy Order baronies, Free Cities, and Merchant Republics (Venice) down the line. Yes, this could create a situation where a count has no vassals, but this has not been an issue in CK2 despite being able to lose all the baronies - reason being that a count could always declare a de-jure holding war on a baron that was vassal to his liege, and did, without interference. If anything, I'd be strongly in favor of this not becoming a hardcoded restriction, even if there are few to none current gameplay ways for a barony to leave the vassalage of the corresponding county.

## Reply 150 — participant_089 · 2019-11-06 · post-25987196

<!-- artifact:quote:start -->
> Servancour said: Hello everyone! I would like to take a moment to talk about the map of Crusader Kings 3, what the vision for the map is, and how it is different from Crusader Kings 2. Let’s start with our ambitions. CK2 had several parts of the map that was outdated, and to be frank, a bit underdeveloped. When we started to update the map for CK3, we knew that we wanted to take a pass at everything, do additional research, and update the different areas accordingly. This goes for the entire De Jure title hierarchy, so there are several new kingdoms and duchies present. In terms of scope, the map will roughly match that of CK2. I know I will disappoint those of you hoping for China, but, sadly, it will not be on the map. We will however, have a few new additions: the entirety of Tibet will be present, unlike CK2 where the most eastern parts were excluded, and sub-Saharan Africa is also extended, where we’ve gone all the way to the Nigerian coast. When setting the map visuals, province layout, rivers, and more, the focus has always been on clarity. The map should be easy to read and get information from. For example, you should be able to read most of the terrain simply by looking at the map, without the need to click on the province, or tooltip it, in order to find that out, while rivers should be easy to see and let you know if you will cross one when moving armies around. We represent the map on three different zoom levels. When zoomed far out, the map will turn into an actual paper map, allowing for an easy overview and stylish screenshots. Zoom in a bit and you will have the 3D map, with the typical political overlay, great for interacting with your vassals and other realms. Zoom in even further and you’ll see the names of all the counties along with the terrain, as we strip away the realm colors. Perfect for moving armies around and knowing where to pick your battles, without the need to switch around to different map modes (but don’t worry, we still have several map modes for easily accessing different information). One of the most notable changes is how we handle Baronies. In CK2, Counties were the smallest entity we had on the map, a province if you will, with several Baronies represented through the interface of the County view. In CK3, we took the next logical step and made Baronies into their own provinces. We have been able to create a map with much more granularity and better accuracy. Most Counties will normally consist of two to five Baronies, with some exceptions. The amount of provinces will be noticeable when waging war, as it offers a larger degree of movement for you armies (more on that in the future). View attachment 523607 To give you a good idea of the increased province density, here is a comparison of the British Islands in CK2 and CK3, being on the left and right side, respectively: View attachment 523608 Before you all go nuts about playable baronies: No. You cannot play as a Baron. The lowest playable rank will still be that of a Count. The emphasis will therefore be on the Counties rather than the individual Baronies. As such, Baronies exist with a few things in mind. For example, they can never leave a county. This means Counties stay the same over time, avoiding weird splits where a single barony goes independent or to another realm (reducing that hideous border-gore ever-so-slightly). The number of Baronies within a County is one factor that represents its wealth and how “good” it is. Another important factor is the terrain. A County with a lot of Desert will not be as beneficial as one with a lot of Farmlands for example. Speaking of terrain, we have several different terrain types spread out across the map. Instead of having a single terrain spread out across large areas of the map, we differentiate between similar terrain types by separating them, such as Forest and Taiga, or Plains and Drylands. Not only does it make the map look and feel distinct in different parts of the world, they also have a different impact on gameplay. View attachment 523605 View attachment 523604 Then we have Impassable Terrain. These are far more frequent, and in many cases much larger, than you will be used to from CK2. We’ve essentially used these for any area that we consider uninhabited enough to warrant it not being part of an existing County. Some areas have plenty of smaller impassable provinces, such as the mountains surrounding Bohemia, while others have fewer and far larger pieces of inhospitable land, such as the deserts of Arabia and Syria. Impassable Terrain cannot be traversed by armies, often creating bottlenecks that you’ll have to pass through or perhaps even choose to go around, should it be heavily fortified. View attachment 523606 That’s it for now. I hope you enjoyed this early sneak peak of the map and I'll be sure to show more to you in the future! Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->

That said, you guys really need to work on those barony and county borders (the dotted and double lines when zoomed in), some terrain types (like forests and mountains) makes it impossible to see them. Maybe make them a different color when passing through such terrains or something, anything to make them visible. That is quite urgent imo.

## Reply 185 — participant_117 · 2019-11-06 · post-25987587

Although i tried to refrain from criticism untill i saw the final product, i'll have to admit that making baronies stuck on their respective county, makes the whole concept of baronies on the map somewhat meaningless. Sure it will allow for better terrain type representation and better strategic value when commanding army movement, which is great, but the ability of molding the borders with a more refined shape is wasted by making counties the smallest amount of conquerable land. Territories which suffered prolonged warfare with small gradual border changes suffer the most from this, first thing that comes to mind is the Reconquista, which was a very slow and gradual process that in CK2 simply cannot be recreated due to the fact the "Holy War" casus beli is duchy wide, and Iberia has a ridiculously low province density (despite having an higher populational density than the British Islands or Germany), meaning that you can finish the Reconquista in ~3/4 wars. It would be far more interesting if we could expand our territories one barony at the time.

## Reply 195 — participant_123 · 2019-11-06 · post-25987666

Isn't the main problem with baronies outside the counties control that it causes you to not fulfill the "completly controls X" trigger? How about not implementing such a trigger (only requiring you to own the top county), instead of making baronies always be vassals of their county? As for the secondary problem: "Nobody wants to go to war over a single barony!" - That's true of course. But the problem aren't the baronies, it's the CB/Peace system which does not allow you to press all dejure claims and your personal claims at the same time. So add a peace deal option (not like in EU4, but restricted to your (and your vassals) claims/dejure claims - why shouldn't you go to war for multiple things?). So you're not only going to war for one single barony anymore. But let's suppose that you only have this one (dejure) claim on the barony. Wouldn't it be annoying in this case? Well, the main cause of being annoyed by this in CK2 is that you will need to disband your levy after each war and raise it again for the next one. This is the main reason why such a small war is a tedious thing. Allow everyone to declare wars with raised levies (if inside own territory). Problem fixed. And one could even add a "threaten war" option for this. So in case your target believes this one barony to be too insignificant and the cost of war too high, it will just give it up, transferring the baron to you.

## Reply 204 — participant_127 · 2019-11-06 · post-25987718

Perhaps only allow the baronies to drift to adjacent counts? A bit ahistoric, yes, but would allow to build some tension between the neighbors. A city de-jure being under one lord, but de-facto the other one is who protects them and collects the taxes, etc. Could lead to feuds too! 2) Going to war over baronies. As someone pointed it out, that is not the problem with the barons, but with the Casus Belli system itself. I am sure, warfare and war diplomacy will be addressed later and I have high hopes fo rit. 3) Free Cities, Holy Orders and other oddballs. I think I would prefer something a bit more unique than them being just some barony, but I can't put a finger on what exactly what it would be. All in all, I really hope in the future we will see baronies expanded and given more focus. And maybe, one day, we will even see landless characters as the ultimate challenge: from rags to riches.

## Reply 222 — participant_137 · 2019-11-06 · post-25987977

Sorry, but this has killed a lot of my interest in the game. Why do we need this much province density? We still can't play as baronies, so why do they each need their own province? You're adding granularity for the sake of granularity at this point, while we still have a third of Eurasia that could be modeled. This also makes modding much more of a pain in the ass, and it also removes a lot of the unique advantages of CKII's holding system. Differences in holding type, number, and upgrades allowed massive differences between provinces, and this could all be changed on the fly in-game. This was meaningful granularity. Now? Unless holdings are no longer tied to barony titles, and you can build multiple per province/barony, the max output of a given county is hardcoded into the map itself. In CKII, I can increase holding number with a simple event, making it a dynamic value that can change through the course of a single game. In CKIII, as far as I know, I might have to make a map mod. And if holdings and holding slots are now just a sub-barony level thing... again, you're just adding an extra level of granularity for nothing. We wanted more provinces. We don't need this many more provinces. Our computers are better today, yes, but how do the gameplay benefits outweigh the costs here? Is a map expansion to China and SE Asia even possible with this level of province density? What about possible CPU-intensive mechanics that you may want to add later, but haven't even thought of yet? And what about our computers - you're increasing the required specs, and decreasing performance even for people who do meet minimum specs. I could put up with that for something meaningful, like China or some great new gameplay mechanic. But why am I sacrificing performance (or the ability to play the game on weaker laptops) so that East Anglia can have a dozen provinces? EDIT: Really, immaterial baronies added a lot of flexibility to the game. You could spawn an extra holding slot and add a holding for a military order, or represent an important temple via an extra temple holding. How will, say, the GoT mod represent the Great Sept as the High Septon's capital holding? Instead of being in the King's Landing province, and us assuming that it's just inside the city, it will have to be given a physical space on the map comparable to King's Landing itself. Likewise if we want to represent the Hagia Sophia in Constantinople...

## Reply 231 — participant_002 · 2019-11-06 · post-25988090

<!-- artifact:quote:start -->
> mudcrabmerchant said: Sorry, but this has killed a lot of my interest in the game. Why do we need this much province density? We still can't play as baronies, so why do they each need their own province? You're adding granularity for the sake of granularity at this point, while we still have a third of Eurasia that could be modeled. This also makes modding much more of a pain in the ass, and it also removes a lot of the unique advantages of CKII's holding system. Differences in holding type, number, and upgrades allowed massive differences between provinces, and this could all be changed on the fly in-game. This was meaningful granularity. Now? Unless holdings are no longer tied to barony titles, and you can build multiple per province/barony, the max output of a given county is hardcoded into the map itself. In CKII, I can increase holding number with a simple event, making it a dynamic value that can change through the course of a single game. In CKIII, as far as I know, I might have to make a map mod. And if holdings and holding slots are now just a sub-barony level thing... again, you're just adding an extra level of granularity for nothing. We wanted more provinces. We don't need this many more provinces. Our computers are better today, yes, but how do the gameplay benefits outweigh the costs here? Is a map expansion to China and SE Asia even possible with this level of province density? What about possible CPU-intensive mechanics that you may want to add later, but haven't even thought of yet? And what about our computers - you're increasing the required specs, and decreasing performance even for people who do meet minimum specs. I could put up with that for something meaningful, like China or some great new gameplay mechanic. But why am I sacrificing performance (or the ability to play the game on weaker laptops) so that East Anglia can have a dozen provinces? EDIT: Also, immaterial baronies added a lot of flexibility to the game. You could spawn an extra holding slot and add a holding for a military order, or represent an important temple via an extra temple holding. How will, say, the GoT mod represent the Great Sept as the High Septon's capital holding? Instead of being in the King's Landing province, and us assuming that it's just inside the city, it will have to be given a physical space on the map comparable to King's Landing itself. Likewise if we want to represent the Hagia Sophia in Constantinople... Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
I'm confident we can say that baronies will play mostly a strategic role, at least at launch, so they won't be just useless. We already have confirmation of choke points and specific bridge locations on rivers, so you'll have to be more careful when moving your troops around. Other than that, PDX has said that baronies will act as "refrigerators" for interesting characters. I think you're right when saying that barony-modding will basically be map-modding, but I don't find problems for specific buildings like Hagia Sophia, the Apostolic Palace, etc. They won't be at launch, it seems, but I'm pretty sure they'll use more or less the same system as Great Works in CK2. Buildings are there too, so I think that you could mod new buildings in if you really want. But once we get a Great Works update it'll be even better.

## Reply 264 — participant_165 · 2019-11-07 · post-25988566

<!-- artifact:quote:start -->
> Servancour said: Hello everyone! I would like to take a moment to talk about the map of Crusader Kings 3, what the vision for the map is, and how it is different from Crusader Kings 2. Let’s start with our ambitions. CK2 had several parts of the map that was outdated, and to be frank, a bit underdeveloped. When we started to update the map for CK3, we knew that we wanted to take a pass at everything, do additional research, and update the different areas accordingly. This goes for the entire De Jure title hierarchy, so there are several new kingdoms and duchies present. In terms of scope, the map will roughly match that of CK2. I know I will disappoint those of you hoping for China, but, sadly, it will not be on the map. We will however, have a few new additions: the entirety of Tibet will be present, unlike CK2 where the most eastern parts were excluded, and sub-Saharan Africa is also extended, where we’ve gone all the way to the Nigerian coast. When setting the map visuals, province layout, rivers, and more, the focus has always been on clarity. The map should be easy to read and get information from. For example, you should be able to read most of the terrain simply by looking at the map, without the need to click on the province, or tooltip it, in order to find that out, while rivers should be easy to see and let you know if you will cross one when moving armies around. We represent the map on three different zoom levels. When zoomed far out, the map will turn into an actual paper map, allowing for an easy overview and stylish screenshots. Zoom in a bit and you will have the 3D map, with the typical political overlay, great for interacting with your vassals and other realms. Zoom in even further and you’ll see the names of all the counties along with the terrain, as we strip away the realm colors. Perfect for moving armies around and knowing where to pick your battles, without the need to switch around to different map modes (but don’t worry, we still have several map modes for easily accessing different information). One of the most notable changes is how we handle Baronies. In CK2, Counties were the smallest entity we had on the map, a province if you will, with several Baronies represented through the interface of the County view. In CK3, we took the next logical step and made Baronies into their own provinces. We have been able to create a map with much more granularity and better accuracy. Most Counties will normally consist of two to five Baronies, with some exceptions. The amount of provinces will be noticeable when waging war, as it offers a larger degree of movement for you armies (more on that in the future). View attachment 523607 To give you a good idea of the increased province density, here is a comparison of the British Islands in CK2 and CK3, being on the left and right side, respectively: View attachment 523608 Before you all go nuts about playable baronies: No. You cannot play as a Baron. The lowest playable rank will still be that of a Count. The emphasis will therefore be on the Counties rather than the individual Baronies. As such, Baronies exist with a few things in mind. For example, they can never leave a county. This means Counties stay the same over time, avoiding weird splits where a single barony goes independent or to another realm (reducing that hideous border-gore ever-so-slightly). The number of Baronies within a County is one factor that represents its wealth and how “good” it is. Another important factor is the terrain. A County with a lot of Desert will not be as beneficial as one with a lot of Farmlands for example. Speaking of terrain, we have several different terrain types spread out across the map. Instead of having a single terrain spread out across large areas of the map, we differentiate between similar terrain types by separating them, such as Forest and Taiga, or Plains and Drylands. Not only does it make the map look and feel distinct in different parts of the world, they also have a different impact on gameplay. View attachment 523605 View attachment 523604 Then we have Impassable Terrain. These are far more frequent, and in many cases much larger, than you will be used to from CK2. We’ve essentially used these for any area that we consider uninhabited enough to warrant it not being part of an existing County. Some areas have plenty of smaller impassable provinces, such as the mountains surrounding Bohemia, while others have fewer and far larger pieces of inhospitable land, such as the deserts of Arabia and Syria. Impassable Terrain cannot be traversed by armies, often creating bottlenecks that you’ll have to pass through or perhaps even choose to go around, should it be heavily fortified. View attachment 523606 That’s it for now. I hope you enjoyed this early sneak peak of the map and I'll be sure to show more to you in the future! Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
Great dev diary! I know this is a little off topic, but are you planning on making any changes to how feudal relationships are represented in game? For instance, in real life Henry II and other English kings were independent monarchs as the kings of England, but as dukes of Normandy and other French duchies they were also vassals of the French king, and were expected to pay homage to him. However, in-game, there's not really anything to represent that, and the King of England is instead wholly independent. Is there going to be any representation for that kind of complicated, nuanced relationship, or are feudal relationship going to be linear as they were in CK2? If it's not something that you've thought about much yet, then I'd like to suggest you do - it's doubtless a tricky feature to work out and implement, but in terms of simulating medieval politics it would be really fantastic, and enthuse the player base about the ways in which CK3 is mechanically superior to CK2.

## Reply 280 — participant_176 · 2019-11-07 · post-25988770

## Reply 281 — participant_174 · 2019-11-07 · post-25988771

<!-- artifact:quote:start -->
> Edward the Brave said: For example, Kiev. How could it be of a russian culture if the Kievan royals founded Moscow in the 13th century which was the start of the russian culture as it is, and so on, and so forth.. Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
Russian in CK2 means a common eastern slavic culture before it split into Ukrainian, Belorussian and what you call "Russian as it is". And since they called themselves Rus, even in Kiev (*), obviously that culture's name would be Russian, even if it might be different from what we know as Russian today. (*) It's even called Kievan Rus in the game, although now that I have a chance let me say that it shouldn't. They didn't call themselves Kievan Rus, just Rus for all the Russian lands or Kievan Principality for lands of Kiev, and Kievan Rus is just a term historians use for the period when the Principality of Kiev happened to be the center of all the Russian lands. However in the game any other Principality can become that or even an external force can conquer these territories, in which cases it makes no sense for all the Principalities be just Principalities (actually they're Kingdoms right now, but they should be Grand Principalities with Duchies being Principalities) while only Kiev somehow being Kievan Rus for no reason. It's like... if you start as a random count in Scotland, conquer all of the Britain, form the Empire and a couple of Kingdoms to fit in the vassal limit, and then discovered what you thought was the Kingdom of England was actually for no particular reason called London's Britain. Even if it isn't Britain, but only one Kingdom of it that historically happened to become the most prominent, but not in your game, and it might even not have its capital in London.

## Reply 300 — participant_190 · 2019-11-07 · post-25989431

The map and new system look good for strategic gameplay. I am excited by the changes. I have three simple questions with regards to the new way baronies and counties are handled. 1) Are county titles still tied to one of the baronies of the county, in the way that county titles are tied to the capital barony of a county in CK2? It would be nice if county titles are no longer tied to an actual barony itself, as this would allow realistic things like a count holding a county title but no baronies in the county itself. For example, suppose the count of York also holds the county of Leicester, but his only barony is in York. A benefit of this system is that it would make it possible to have county level vice-royalties in bureaucratic realms. 2) If county titles are still tied to the capital barony, can capital baronies be changed, or are we stuck with whatever the capital is at the beginning of the game. 3) Since baron titles are always held by vassals of the count whose county they are in, what happens if you grant a county to one of your barons? Does his barony title return to you?

## Reply 304 — participant_016 · 2019-11-07 · post-25989656

To add a few things to the barony discussion. As stated already, baronies will always be considered to be a part of a county. However, we do have a concept of leasing baronies to other rulers, such as Holy Orders. This effectively gives them control of the holding along with the income it generates, without it "leaving" your realm. We'll go into further details in the future.

## Reply 318 — participant_200 · 2019-11-07 · post-25990139

<!-- artifact:quote:start -->
> Servancour said: The former. You cannot transfer barony vassals out of a given county, and the county holder will always be that baron's liege. Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
This is a huge shame, in my view, since it renders the whole scheme of William I of England to control his vassals and bind the kingdom under him impossible. His splitting of the land vassals held around the kingdom, both to force them to keep itinerant courts and to divide their military forces in the event of an uprising, was crucial to his success in holding a newly conquered "empire". This, together with the shifts in inheritance rules that were happening in the 11th century, marked a seismic change in medieval English society, just for one example.

## Reply 319 — participant_201 · 2019-11-07 · post-25990142

As a theorycraft, you could have de jure counties that shift as you conquer baronies. There could be a cap on the amount of baronies in a county, or simply a chance for a county of large size to split on ruler death as added inheritance interest. If a county were fully conquered, it would be possible to have it disappear as de jure if it didn’t exist for a century. If you were a baron who managed to expand their realm, could declare war on your county liege(s) to carve out your own county. This would emphasize areas shifting in importance as time passes, as different baronies are lavished interest upon by different counts. Just a thought. Certainly a lot of room to think. Mod support for this sort of expanded gameplay would be nice if implemented somewhere down the line.

## Reply 320 — participant_202 · 2019-11-07 · post-25990159

I understand the decision not to make baronies playable, if only because if baronies receive the level of events the player has to be exposed to for a good gameplay experience, you have nearly 5 times as many character events being fired at any given time and I can see a lot of computers struggling with that. I don't necessarily endorse it, but I think it is definitely an acceptable decision if the game runs well and is smooth. I also kind of buy the argument about the game being "lacking" if you were a baron because you have no real vassal management yourself. That's fair. However, I really very strongly disagree with the decision to make sure that baronies are always tied to the same county. This is frustrating because, as mentioned by others, it really goes against the way the medieval era actually functioned. From Free Cities in the Holy Roman Empire, to the merchant republics of Venice and Genoa that only ever really controlled coastal cities and not their hinterlands, to tiny little outposts of feudal territory like Avignon or Calais which are definitely barony-sized and not county-sized in real life - the medieval life was one predominantly consisting of bordergore. It is really odd to see the game not reflect that, especially when the reason given is "we didn't like bordergore". That's basically saying "we didn't like the medieval era being what it was", which is a bad attitude to take towards CK3 (in my view). The real solution to bordergore of the un-medieval/artificial sort was twofold: firstly, in developing a smarter and more dynamic exclave independence system, and secondly, allowing multiple claims to be pushed in a single war (basically a more EU4 war deal system) so that stray baronies didn't cause a mega-war then 10 years of forced peace with no other gains, which was admittedly really annoying in CK2. I really cannot stress how much I do not like the decision to instead just stick baronies permanently with counties and throw away so much opportunity for emergent gameplay.

## Reply 359 — participant_027 · 2019-11-07 · post-25991223

<!-- artifact:quote:start -->
> Larz1695 said: That is a lot of provinces. My first thought is great! More land to give to my dynasty! My second is whether or not I will need to rent server time from NASA in order to play this game. Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
It's quite deceptive. The developers have said that in terms of actual provinces/counties, the map won't be too much bigger than CK II's. The reasons it looks like there are loads more provinces is because baronies now physically exist on the map, and have their own borders. They're still part of counties, however, and counties remain the smallest independent entity on the map. They will still be the 'provinces' you click on, it seems. So, for example, if you compare the two maps of the British Isles you'd think there's been a huge increase in the number of counties/provinces, but there hasn't.

From what you're saying you still think the provinces will have the ability to have multiple holdings. That's a no. This is what the province view in CKII is currently like (with HIP) What the devs have done is removed all of the sub-holdings, as in the vassals of a province from the province and made them their own provinces. So for example, that castle with the boat banner would be it's own province, not a sub-holding anymore, it'll be on the map as it's own place. So unless you want to change the map each time you "add a holding" to a county, there will be a fixed number of holdings and provinces. Obviously aside from provinces that hold nothing, you are unable to build more

## Reply 382 — participant_134 · 2019-11-07 · post-25991945

<!-- artifact:quote:start -->
> Dusty242 said: Now my historic knowledge on the Maghreb region is rather lax compared to other regions in this period, and as I doubt the Clans of the Maghreb were anything like the Scottish Clans so that's probably a bad comparison. What would you recommend as tribal mechanics for the Maghreb? Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
<!-- artifact:quote:start -->
> elvain said: @Trinita132 I have somehow mixed impressions about your posts. I tend to agree with you that the Maghreb and the entire Arab-Muslim world should function differently than Western Europe (heck, see my signature for my suggestions about improving CK2 Muslim world and experience as muslim player), I just somehow don't share your negativity when it comes to your comments. What makes you feel that the map does not reflect tribes? I don't have the impression that the map doesn't respect the situation of medieval Maghreb. Based on my extensive research of Maghrebi history I made a map of medieval Maghreb's important regions, towns, oases and cities and I believe that what we've seen so far doesn't seem to miss anything important. I'm not showing various placement of the tribes, which were moving here and there across those regions, but many of the places shown on this map are centers of various tribes and I don't feel like the map shown by the devs doesn't respect the distribution of tribes. Rather the contrary... Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
Nothing really special, in a simplified way : Each tribe should have : A manpower pool that shows it strength, a chief which represents the tribe, relations with nearby tribes (warm, neutral, execrable) which may lead to tribal feuding. Tribal feuding = You are at war and you can raid the enemy tribe. If a tribe has enough strenght, it can try to form a "confederation" by inviting nearby tribes to join. Each "confederation" must have a goal (Support a pretender, independance, seize power, ...), unity (between 0 and 100) and the strongest tribe will take the leadership. After the phase of creation, unity will decrease and at 0 the "confederation" will disband, to prevent that they must start the war to fufill their goal, the more they win battles the more the unity increases, a charismatic leader may also slow unity's decrease. I think it would represent better the civil war after sultan's deth, the ascension of dynasties like Almoravid and Almohad, . I have no problem with this map. My opinion is just that it's better to represent tribes (only the strongest because it's impossible to represent all the tribes) at barony's level. Each holding capital must be a city although. This doesn't mean that cities can't be represented at barony's level too. Do you know what I keep talking about tribes? Because they shaped the history of the country. ## Reply 384 — participant_202 · 2019-11-07 · post-25992077

@elvain The other big issue of course is that the iqta is just portayed very poorly in CK2, for almost identical reasons to why the Roman Empire is so poorly portrayed (because the actually used very similar fiscal systems, largely because the early Muslim conquests just adopted existing Roman taxation systems in a lot of cases). CK2 only allows for joint military-civil structure - the owner of a holding/liege of a holding both a) receive the taxes/rents on the land they own, and b) control the soldiers raised from the land they own. The trouble is that only really works in the context of non-Roman Europe (and even then only those bits from Poland and eastwards, at least for most of the timeframe). In the Roman Empire, these were two very clearly distinct systems. The army was heavily regionalised (in the themes), and a strategos commanded the soldiers who worked the land in each regional theme. However, the strategos did not levy taxes on his regional area, and did not receive rents from land worked by peasants. Instead, these were raised by a separate tax official, and went directly to the Imperial court in Constantinople. Instead of getting their income from the land, a strategos received a salary from Constantinople. The iqta worked very similarly - if anything, it was even more similar to the older Roman system before Heraklios, because while the Roman Empire in Anatolia and Greece had heavily regionalised armies which look a little bit like feudal armies in terms of being raised from peasntry (albeit through different mechanisms), the major Muslim powers had mamluk armies composed of slaves who absolutely did not work the land and look more like the old imperial field armies of the Roman Empire before Heraklios. This separation meant the political dynamics of these polities very different - the Western feudal systems were concerned with control of land, which meant decentralised polities where magnates closely guarded their local landholdings, and most courts were itinerant, with kings travelling from region to region to visit these decentralised landlords. Meanwhile, the Roman Empire and most Muslim states were concerned with control of money, which meant hyper-centralised courts where everyone competed for Imperial favour to receive the right to become a tax administrator (or to become the new emperor/sultan). Court intrigue was a much greater focus. If you want to do these regions well, you therefore need each county/barony to have two holders, not one - a military official and a civil official. Arguably, this system would actually be useful in Western Europe as well, allowing for temporal and spiritual authorities to exist in the same province (church dioceses overlapped with landholdings rather than being entirely independent of them; the church would extract tithe from the same land a landholder extracted rent from), but the main use would definitely be outside Western Europe. The Roman Empire later on started looking a lot more like the feudal system after the system of pronoia was introduced. Here, the Imperial court would grant the right to fiscal revenues (i.e., not the right to collect it on behalf of Constantinople, but the right to actually collect it and keep it for yourself) to people in exchange for services, often (albeit not exclusively military). It became common for military leaders for the themes to bid for the various fiscal revenues for the area they governed, and then you end up with a system very similar to Western feudalism (a few differences, but probably ones you can abstract away from simplicity). Certainly after Alexios Komnenos, I think you can probably just handwave the Roman Empire as feudal and apply the Western system, and while it's not perfect it is good enough. But to do the Roman Empire before that, and to do the iqta properly, you need to develop a different system.

## Reply 409 — participant_231 · 2019-11-08 · post-25994769

<!-- artifact:quote:start -->
> Astephos said: Technically it can be the same depending on how you measure shore length Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
No, that's both a different mathematical issue and the wrong answer. The apparent sizes of different territories on a flat map depend on the choice of projection. Gauss's Theorema Egregium implies that no flat map can fairly represent all distances, even at small scales. Consequently, no equal-area map is consistently shape-preserving. If you want Iceland and Iberia to appear the correct sizes relative to each other, at least one of them won't look a sensible shape. The Coastline Paradox, on the other hand, describes the problem that the length of a coastline depends on the length of the ruler used to measure it - the shorter the ruler, the longer the coastline. If it were not for variable factors like tides and erosion, it would be possible to define measures by which coastline could be measured as arbitrarily long - functionally infinite. But that has no effect on the area bounded by the coastline. It's entirely possible for an infinite line to bound a finite area. The Koch Snowflake, for example, can be circumscribed by a simple circle, and thus has a smaller area than it, but is infinitely long. This paradox has no practical impact on everyday cartography. nd

Meanwhile, the Roman Empire and most Muslim states were concerned with control of money, which meant hyper-centralised courts where everyone competed for Imperial favour to receive the right to become a tax administrator (or to become the new emperor/sultan). Court intrigue was a much greater focus. If you want to do these regions well, you therefore need each county/barony to have two holders, not one - a military official and a civil official. Arguably, this system would actually be useful in Western Europe as well, allowing for temporal and spiritual authorities to exist in the same province (church dioceses overlapped with landholdings rather than being entirely independent of them; the church would extract tithe from the same land a landholder extracted rent from), but the main use would definitely be outside Western Europe. The Roman Empire later on started looking a lot more like the feudal system after the system of pronoia was introduced. Here, the Imperial court would grant the right to fiscal revenues (i.e., not the right to collect it on behalf of Constantinople, but the right to actually collect it and keep it for yourself) to people in exchange for services, often (albeit not exclusively military). It became common for military leaders for the themes to bid for the various fiscal revenues for the area they governed, and then you end up with a system very similar to Western feudalism (a few differences, but probably ones you can abstract away from simplicity). Certainly after Alexios Komnenos, I think you can probably just handwave the Roman Empire as feudal and apply the Western system, and while it's not perfect it is good enough. But to do the Roman Empire before that, and to do the iqta properly, you need to develop a different system. Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
You're right but it's in fact even little more complex. What you describe is correct for the big empires. But at the same time there were smaller realms which didn't have the resources and worked little differently and were based on tribal structure. You're right about the dual administration of the empires. The question is how to implement it without adding too much complexity? There has to be a way to simulate a dynastic play (because this game is about dynasties even more than CK2, which I find very positive) and enable dynasty to live also without holding land. What we have seen so far looks like fairly positive and promissing - things like council positions being more of offices than persons etc. Perhaps we could get bureaucratic government in which provinces could be governed by paid officials and dynasties would struggle for those offices... and ducal level titles would also have their special military commanders (strategoi, emirs) being tied to the duchy? And these ducal commanders (emirs, strategoi, but perhaps also dukes in the west) with military power should tend to gain control of the duchy for themselves, take the revenues and become hereditary feudal rulers, thus eroding the bureaucratic empires into something closer to feudal system... But looking at examples from the Muslim empires, I think the only challenge to the central power came from the military commanders, the emirs, or later the atabegs etc. They were the ones who tended to usurp the power and used their military power for that. It was the same when the Ghaznawids established themselves as independent emirs of Samanids, it was how the Zengids, Salghurids and other atabegs used their power to split the Seljuk empire or how Zirids seceded from Fatimid power and later the Hammadids seceeded from Zirid kingdom. That's one challenge. The other were the tribes. They often stood behind the rise of various dynasties as well as behind their downfall, once the dynasty failed to comply with the demands of their own tribesmen, who were the military base of their power. Those were the examples given by @Trinita132 the Lamtuna behind Almoravids, the various Masmuda tribes behind Almohads... Hillalian confederation, Seljuk tribe, Shabankara Kurds as well as the Kutama who were the source of early Fatimid power. Just like the military generals, these tribes should IMHO stay somehow be separated from the government and not be directly attached to land, but instead work as relatively independent power which needs to be employed and its leaders demand offices. By relatively independent I mean that their military power should not entirely depend on land, but some sort of authority or other power. A land/office would be something they demand as reward for their military service, and something what can potentially strenghten their military power. If the tribe/general is not given any land/office, it/he rebels against the ruler. If the rebelling tribe/general is the only or main military power of the ruler, he's doomed. Larger empires will naturaly need to have more such military units (Great Seljuks used their own Seljuk tribe, the Ghuzz Turks, Georgian, Armenian and Daylamite mercenaries/ghulams; Fatimids had the Kutama Berber confederation, the Saqaliba-Slavic, the Abid-Black and later also Turkic slave armies, the Abbasids used various Turkic generals). The basic need of the emperors (Seljuk Great Sultans, Fatimid or Almohad Caliphs etc.) would be to create ballance between those military forces and territorial generals - the emirs, the basic need of medium sized emirates such as Samanid Khorasan, Buyid Persia, Zengid Syria etc. would be to create ballance between various generals. The basic needs of Tribal empires such as the Almoravids, Hilalians, Merinids etc. would be to find a force to ballance the power of their own tribe in case of other clans demanding too much/becomming too powerfull. They all would gain their financial power from holding land and controlling it via civil administration, but if I remember correctly, the civil administration never really presented a threat/challenge to their power and hence should be represented as something what doesn't require too much micromanagement. OTOH, as mentioned above, the bureaucratic officers should tend to demand their offices to be hereditary, eroding the imperial bureaucracy towards the pronoi/iqta semi-feudal fief system (from emperor's perspective creating a landed military force to ballance power of the generals or tribes) This is all based on my observations of Islamic history and some basic knowledge of Byzantine system, which I suppose would be somehow similar... Let's see what we can get...

## Reply 429 — participant_157 · 2019-11-09 · post-25999212

I have noticed some people are displeased with the fact that baronies are tied to counties. I am going to try and explain why this is a reasonable limitation. If baronies were not to be tied to counties, then that means a county could be completely consumed by a neighbouring county. A single county now holds up to 10 or more baronies. This single powerful county takes up only 1 demesne slot. This means a single duke could have 3 uber counties and be the liege of something like 30-40 baronies. This duke would be incredibly powerful with a heavily centralised power base all with a demesne limit of only 3. This duke had to pass no centralisation laws at all to become very centralised. A great administrative and political feat is then achieved with no administrative or political reform at all. Instead, all it took was the conquering of a few nearby baronies. Also, bear in mind, this duke hasn’t even used up any of their vassal limit yet. Okay then, why not just limit the number of baronies that could be held by a single count. Let’s say, 5? The room for geo-political wiggle room in counties is now fairly limited already. Yes, you could make one of your counties of 2 baronies stronger by taking 3 neighbouring baronies. But what if 2 of those 3 baronies were an entire county? Now a county is destroyed, never to return. Suddenly, after let’s say 100 years, the number of total counties in the game is reduced by 20% because of events like this. The entirety of Feudal Europe has become 20% more centralised than it had been 100 years ago not because of administrative reform, but because of warfare. You could say that this isn’t a bad thing, but you could also say that it is. I would say that it is. Of course, de jure county titles could be made in an attempt to remedy some of this problem. This way you could recreate county titles according to de jure hierarchy. But why would you split a county of 4 baronies into 2 de jure counties of 2 baronies? What would ever be the purpose of that? I know the AI sure as hell wouldn’t do that. Now, with this county limit of 5 baronies, what happens after let’s say, 200 years, when all the counties approach the county limit of 5 baronies? Now, every county is ‘as good’ as the other, and the diverse county configuration present at the start of the game is utterly destroyed. What happens when a small pocket of land has been butchered so badly that a sect of 1 barony counties remain surrounded by a bunch of 5 barony counties. Imagine they are all part of the same realm, and under the AI’s control. The AI has a demesne limit of 4. 2 of their 4 demesnes are these 1 baron counties. Obviously, this is not desirable, and this AI is nowhere near their potential power base. However, the AI does not realise this, as they are at their demesne limit, and they believe themselves to be as powerful as they possibly could be in that moment in time. For the AI to solve their problem, they would have to revoke their vassals’ baronies adjacent to their 1 baron counties until their 1 baron counties became 5 baron counties. Getting an AI to cause this much civil strife in order to fill their petty counties’ ‘demesne’ would be much more difficult than getting them to fill their regular demesne. This would be a lot of work on the developer’s shoulders. And what’s the payoff for this extra work? Not a great deal honestly. I hope this brought to light some of the issues that would arise from untying barons to counties.

## Reply 430 — participant_204 · 2019-11-10 · post-25999277

<!-- artifact:quote:start -->
> Consul of Rome said: I have noticed some people are displeased with the fact that baronies are tied to counties. I am going to try and explain why this is a reasonable limitation. If baronies were not to be tied to counties, then that means a county could be completely consumed by a neighbouring county. A single county now holds up to 10 or more baronies. So, now, a count is as powerful as a duke who holds 2 regular counties, and the ‘feudal’ system starts to break down. Regularly, a duke hands out a few county titles, and his counts then hand out a few barony titles. The distribution is appropriate to both geographical and administrative limitations. Now, instead, a count is the holder of up to a dozen or more baronies who all pay fealty to him, regardless of any the aforementioned limitations. This starts to make the title of ‘Duke’ seem insignificant and harms the feudal power ‘dynamic’. Now, this single powerful county takes up only 1 demesne slot. This means a single duke could have 3 uber counties and be the liege of something like 30-40 baronies. This duke would be incredibly powerful with a heavily centralised power base all with a demesne limit of only 3. This duke had to pass no centralisation laws at all to become very centralised. A great administrative and political feat is then achieved with no administrative or political reform at all. Instead, all it took was the conquering of a few nearby baronies. Also, bear in mind, this duke hasn’t even used up any of their vassal limit yet. Okay then, why not just limit the number of baronies that could be held by a single count. Let’s say, 5? The room for geo-political wiggle room in counties is now fairly limited already. Yes, you could make one of your counties of 2 baronies stronger by taking 3 neighbouring baronies. But what if 2 of those 3 baronies were an entire county? Now a county is destroyed, never to return. Suddenly, after let’s say 100 years, the number of total counties in the game is reduced by 20% because of events like this. The entirety of Feudal Europe has become 20% more centralised than it had been 100 years ago not because of administrative reform, but because of warfare. You could say that this isn’t a bad thing, but you could also say that it is. I would say that it is. You could say "isn’t this what happens in CKII anyway? Dukes fill their demesne limit by taking counties and hence destroy counts in order to do so." However, the county still remains, and so does the title of the county. If the aforementioned scenario were to occur, county titles would be destroyed altogether, and no trace of the county would remain. So yes, these are extremely different circumstances (I know, a bit obvious, but whatever, just in case someone thought this). Of course, de jure county titles could be made in an attempt to remedy some of this problem. This way you could recreate county titles according to de jure hierarchy. But why would you split a county of 4 baronies into 2 de jure counties of 2 baronies? What would ever be the purpose of that? I know the AI sure as hell wouldn’t do that. I hope this brought to light some of the issues that would arise from untying barons to counties. Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
The problem is, is that the system in CKIII with baronies static to counties means that it'd be like having a duke in CKII who could only take other dukedoms, no single county takeover allowed. Now, you could have it similar to CKII in the fact you need like 50% of a county's baronies to usurp or create a county title, but that doesn't make much sense, as a county is already small enough to be a solidly defined area, while duchies are much more prone to fluctuate in size. Also I don't think you really understand how power centralization works. You say that a single duke could personally hold 3 counties while lording over 30-40 barons, which would mean power isn't centralized, it's distributed 30-40 times. If a duke held every single holding within that duchy, that'd be centralized power. In CKIII, barons are now the lowest form of holding on the map, unlike CKII, so holding 3 county titles as a duke in CKIII would be like holding 3 duchy titles as a king in CKII without holding any of the counties in those duchies. To hold a county in CKII you need to hold the top barony, but to hold a duchy you don't need to personally hold any of the land from that duchy if you already have the title. In CKIII though, if you wanted, you could hold the county titles like duchies from CKII and delegate the land to barons. I doubt stewardship and demense size will be changing very much, so there'd be no problem holding one county title and taking as many baronies as you want, due to the ability to hand out anything over your demense to barons, thus de-centralizing power. Though we're unable to do this, as I refer back to my earlier point about how it'd be like dukes only being able to take dukedoms. Also not to mention, dukes can swallow entirely different duchies and not have a problem with power creep, like you're suggesting counts would do if they could take other whole other counties, since they'd be able to hand out the land, reducing their power

## Reply 431 — participant_157 · 2019-11-10 · post-25999319

<!-- artifact:quote:start -->
> Astephos said: Also I don't think you really understand how power centralization works. You say that a single duke could personally hold 3 counties while lording over 30-40 barons, which would mean power isn't centralized, it's distributed 30-40 times. If a duke held every single holding within that duchy, that'd be centralized power. Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
Yes, it is distributed 30-40 times, but those barons pay tax/levy to that one duke. That means the duke is direct liege to 30-40 sub-holding vassals. Regularly, if a duke were to have 3 counties in CKII, they would be the direct liege to around 9 sub-holding vassals. In CKII, if a count is your vassal, that county's sub-holding vassals pay direct levy/tax to the count. Then, that count pays direct tax/levy to you. You only get a fraction of what the count earns, and the count only gets a fraction of what the sub-holding vassals earn. So, if a count's city earns 40 gold a year, that count only gets 10 of that, and in turn you only get 2.5 of that. If that city paid directly to you, you would get 10 gold a year. Moreover, if that count is a 'feudal vassal' (a castle vassal), you do not see a single coin earned by him or his sub-holding vassals. By having 30-40 direct sub-holdings at a single time without having the need for the count middleman, your income and levy is increased ridiculously. That's what I meant by 'centralisation'; a whole rung on the feudal ladder is removed (the count), and the barons pay you directly. There is a big difference between a duke distributing land to counts who then distribute land to barons, rather than a duke distributing land just to barons.

I mean, in reality dukes hardly ever had counts as "vassals", they'd have knights and lords who'd swear their fealty and be granted a fief or a lordship to govern on behalf of the duke, sometimes gaining enough power to be recognized formally as a baron by the crown, or in terms of the HRE, the ducal crown. So a duke holding 3 "counties" and having 30-40 lords or barons under him would be possible though smaller amounts would be more commonplace. Though I doubt it'd be close to 30-40 vassals with only 3 counties, as there are no "sub-holdings" in CKIII, every holding is now on the map, so 3 counties would be around 8-12 baronies/towns/churches

## Reply 434 — participant_157 · 2019-11-10 · post-25999425

<!-- artifact:quote:start -->
> Astephos said: Though I doubt it'd be close to 30-40 vassals with only 3 counties, as there are no "sub-holdings" in CKIII, every holding is now on the map, so 3 counties would be around 8-12 baronies/towns/churches Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
Yes, currently, 3 counties would be around 8-12 baronies/towns/churches in CKIII, which is a good spot. I was trying to describe why the scenario in which baronies/towns/churches are not tied to counties is bad because of the fact that you could potentially have 30-40 baronies/towns/churches within 3 counties. As you said, historically this may have been possible but from a gameplay perspective I don't find it very attractive. If one was able to grow their counties unchecked by adding baronies to them without limit (as one would be able to do if baronies were no longer tied to a single county); they could become immensely powerful with a minimal demesne size. The gameplay would just devolve into a 'conquer-barony' state where you simply make claims on nearby baronies, add them to your closest county through warfare, become stronger through the tax/levy provided directly to you by that barony/town/church, then rinse and repeat. It'd harm many of the features and focuses of game and it'd be fairly similar to how one would play EUIV.

## Reply 435 — participant_235 · 2019-11-10 · post-25999550

<!-- artifact:quote:start -->
> Consul of Rome said: If one was able to grow their counties unchecked by adding baronies to them without limit (as one would be able to do if baronies were no longer tied to a single county); they could become immensely powerful with a minimal demesne size. The gameplay would just devolve into a 'conquer-barony' state where you simply make claims on nearby baronies, add them to your closest county through warfare, become stronger through the tax/levy provided directly to you by that barony/town/church, then rinse and repeat. It'd harm many of the features and focuses of game and it'd be fairly similar to how one would play EUIV. Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
Wait a second, do directly owned baronies not count as part of your demesne anymore? I could've sworn they did in CK2.

## Reply 436 — participant_157 · 2019-11-10 · post-25999666

<!-- artifact:quote:start -->
> Fulbert said: Wait a second, do directly owned baronies not count as part of your demesne anymore? I could've sworn they did in CK2. Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
No, they do. However baron vassals do not count towards your vassal limit. So, in the scenario I described, you could easily have a very large amount of baron vassals who pay their income directly to you. This was impossible to do in CKII because counties had a limit to their sub-holdings. If you had 3 counties and your demesne limit was 3, you would have around 10 sub-holding vassals in your counties paying direct tribute to you. If you wanted to try and grow your power base from there, you would then have to try and get some vassal counts/dukes or increase your demesne limit. Now, in the scenario I was trying to describe before, where baronies are not tied to counties, all you would have to do in a situation like this to grow your power base would be to conquer a neighbouring barony and add it to one of your counties. Hence, the incentive to acquire vassals above the rank of baron (and contribute to your vassal limit) is almost completely gone. Just like that, a nice feature of the game pretty much becomes irrelevant. What would the point be of having county vassals when their barony vassals pay tribute to them rather than you. Mathematically, it'd be much more efficient to have as many of the baronies in your realm be a part of your counties as possible. Just to give you an idea of how much power direct baron vassals provide you, i'll give you some numbers. Imagine you have 40 direct baron/bishopry/city vassals. 20 of them are cities of a moderate city income of around 40/year. These city vassals with the default tax obligation of 25% would provide you 16.67 gold/month. Let's say the other 20 are barons (castles). Every one of them has 1000 levies. They all have a 50 opinion of you. With no crown authority, and the default feudal levy obligation, they would provide you with 3000 levies. With just a demesne limit of 3, and no vassals (that count towards your vassal limit) whatsoever, you could have roughly 7000 levies and 20 gold/month. This is also before any considerable province improvements have been made. Yeah that's cool, but what's the point of it. It would result in the player simply taking baronies the entire game without having to ever consider any significant domestic intrigue/strife because of the lack of any actual real vassals. Like I said, it'd be like playing EUIV. Sorry for the long answer by the way
